In this Bill is contained wording about how the county AND school board could each impose a 1/2 cent sales surtax...WITH A SUPERMAJORITY SCHOOL BOARD VOTE! (5 yea,2 nea)\n \nSo while I was researching information on the SAP/R3 computer system (a much, too much money pit!) I ran across something very interesting about this \"surtax\" that I was not fully aware of...and I would bet that the general public doesn't know either. This proposed surtax in SB382 refers to Capital Expenses ONLY!\n \nCapital money is used primarily to pay for building construction and renovation, but not building maintenance. Capital money also covers the cost of other big-ticket items such as school buses and computers (SAP/R3).\nUnlike county and municipal governments, school districts cannot mix capital and operating expenses. Operating funds cover the day-to-day expense of running the school district. Salaries, supplies, student transportation, and building maintenance are all included in the operating budget. \n \nThis means NONE of this money derived from the surtax could be used for anything other than capital expenses...new schools and school renovations! NO WAGE INCREASES FROM THIS TAX>>>PERIOD!\nSo, if you thought that by supporting a surtax increase for the school district would help the teacher wage problem, or help in reducing the student/teacher ratio, or help in keeping those truant officers employed in order to reduce the dropout rates, or any thing else you might have thought...well your WRONG!\n \nThis Bill WILL help build more schools, and make the building of those new schools better because corners will not have to be cut because of funding. It will also help in getting rid of those ugly portable classrooms we all hate. And the gyms, music buildings, cafeterias, and the like could also be built where needed. But what this will ultimately do is create unusable square footage of building space...BECAUSE...the state continues to short school districts on operating funding! AND the school district's school board continues to go unchecked with their un-satisfiable appetite for spending operating funds in a crazy manner.\n \nSorry Polk County Teachers and support personel...no raise for you even if this bad legislation AS WRITTEN does pass. Pg. 1B, 5B\n\nLENGTH: 865 words\n\nHEADLINE: Fishermen thwart shark study; Fisheries officials say the outcome of\n  the next assessent is uncertain\n\nBYLINE: Cory Reiss, Washington Correspondent\n\nBODY:\n\n\n    WASHINGTON | Commercial fishermen helped sink a government study of the\n  ocean's top predators - sharks - to challenge the assumption that sharks\nare in\n  danger and to curb cuts in fishing quotas.\n\n    Fishermen expect the recent defeat of a 1998 study showing sharks in\ndecline\n  will result in a new assessment supporting their argument: That\nincreasing shark\n  attacks are because of more sharks in the water.\n\n    \"The industry has been victimized over sharks by different groups that had\n  certain agendas, and what we were seeing was people getting bitten,\" said\nRobert\n  Spaeth, executive director of the Southern Offshore Fisheries Association and\n  other plaintiffs that blocked new quotas.\n\n    Sharks, which attacked 52 people in United States waters last year, 36 of\n  them in Florida, evoked fear among tourists with several highly publicized\n  attacks, including a bull shark that ripped the arm off an 8-year-old boy\nnear\n  Pensacola. The attention rekindled disputes between fishermen and\n  conservationists, who argue there are more recreational swimmers, not more\n  sharks.\n\n    In 2000, worldwide shark attacks reached a record 85. Florida, North\nCarolina\n  and Alabama hit records of 38, five and two, respectively.\n\n    Quotas, which already had been cut in half since federal management\nbegan in\n  1993, will remain at 1997 levels for the season that began Jan. 1. The\ndispute\n  only affects commercial fishermen in the Atlantic and Gulf of Mexico,\nwhere most\n  shark fishing and most shark attacks occur.\n\n    Fisheries officials say the outcome of the next assessment, which may be\n  complete in April, is uncertain.  \"The modeling may give us a different\npicture\n  on where sharks are and where sharks have been,\" said Margo Schulze-Haugen, a\n  government fisheries biologist.\n\n    Conservation groups denounced the decision, saying fishermen hijacked a\n  process meant to guard against overfishing. They worried the episode\nwould fuel\n  challenges to other conservation efforts while allowing depletion of shark\n  stocks.\n\n    \"I would think the industry would be pretty encouraged by what happened,\"\n  said Ellen Pikitch, director of marine programs for the Wildlife Conservation\n  Society.\n\n    Fishermen plan to use this case as proof that fisheries management needs an\n  overhaul and more scientific oversight. Leaders of at least two large fishing\n  associations plan a lobbying trip to Washington next month.\n\n    \"It gives us hope that the system will be improved and that the scientific\n  work on which so much of the regulations are based will be given much better\n  consideration,\" Southeastern Fisheries Association Director Bob Jones said.\n\n    The independent scientists concluded that the 1998 study wasn't based\non the\n  best science or that inappropriate models were used. A principle target\nof their\n  criticism was a population model suggested by the three Wildlife Conservation\n  Society members on the 22-member assessment panel, prompting one independent\n  reviewer to question the objectivity of the process.\n\n    Meanwhile, experts on both sides predict more attacks this summer. The\nreason\n  depends on the source.\n\n    Fishermen argue that sharp declines in shark fishing since state and\nfederal\n  restrictions took effect in the early 1990s correspond to the attack\nincrease.\n\n    Mr. Jones said he has asked Florida Gov. Jeb Bush to allow commercial\n  fishermen to hunt sharks near shore or to use shark nets around public\nbeaches.\n  Florida, where nearly 60 percent of the nation's federal commercial\npermits are\n  held, has practically banned commercial shark fishing within state waters and\n  limited recreational catches since 1992.\n\n    \"It seems to me that they ought to be at least thinking about it instead of\n  sitting with their fingers crossed hoping people don't get chewed up this\nyear,\"\n  Mr. Jones said.  \"It is almost a death blow to Florida's tourist economy.\"\n\n    In the 1950's, when reporting was much spottier than it is now, there were\n  about 100 recorded attacks worldwide. In the 1990's there were 536.\n\n    George Burgess, director of the International Shark Attack File at the\n  University of Florida in Gainesville, points to data showing that steady\n  population growth in Florida and the world's other shark attack hotspots\n  parallels the increase.\n\n    He concedes that shark populations have rebounded since management efforts\n  began, but slowly. Mr. Burgess said a coastal population boom is causing\nmost of\n  the problem.\n\n    \"Humans, frankly, are flooding sharks out of their own water,\" he said.\n\n    The reviewers made suggestions about how to conduct the population\nstudy. For\n  example, they said, sharks differ from other fish because they mature slowly,\n  often reaching breeding age after 10 or 20 years, and that should be\ntaken into\n  account. Another issue is counting a population that roams widely.\n\n    Experts agree, however, that age and migration data isn't available for\nmost\n  sharks, which remain relatively mysterious.\n\n    For conservationists, what's at stake is the ability to conserve\nspecies when\n  evidence of low numbers exists but scientific data isn't complete.\n\n    \"If the way people react to uncertain science is to do nothing, that means\n  we're setting a precedent to allow fish stocks go down the tubes,\" Ms.\nPikitch\n  said.\n\n\n\nGRAPHIC: ROBERT HOLST / NYTRENG (chart)\n\nLOAD-DATE: January 8, 2002\n\n------ End of Forwarded Message\n\n\nContent-Transfer-Encoding: base64\nContent-Disposition: attachment; \n McKay sent chief of staff Greg Krasovsky to deliver the news.\n\n\"I made the decision that I thought we needed a change in that\nposition,\"\nMcKay said. \"Those are personnel issues that aren't supposed to be in\nthe\npress.\"\n\nIt was McKay's pleasure to fire the staff director at the Senate Ethics\nand\nElections Committee after all of her years of service. Before her chair\nwas\ncold, the job was filled by Wayne Rubinas, a lawyer who happened be a\nhigh\nschool classmate of McKay's. But McKay hastens to add that he wasn't\njust\ntrying to find a job for a friend.\n\nSome legislative staffers saw a connection between Bradshaw's dismissal\nand\nopposition to McKay's tax plan by Sen. Debby Sanderson, R-Fort\nLauderdale.\nSanderson is the first Republican to publicly oppose McKay's plan and is\nchairwoman of the committee where Bradshaw worked.\n\n\"As God is my judge, it had absolutely nothing to do with this,\" McKay\nsaid,\nreferring to his tax plan.\n\nSanderson, visibly upset, said she doesn't know why Bradshaw was ousted,\nand\nlearned of it only five minutes before it happened when Krasovsky told\nher\nMcKay had \"lost confidence\" in Bradshaw.\n\nMcKay said he will try to help her find another state job.\n\nBradshaw has spent her entire career in state government working with\nFlorida election laws, first at the State Division of Elections, then in\nthe\nHouse and for the past five years in the Senate.\n\nThe Legislature has some of the state's best workers hidden away in\noffices\nwho frequently burn the midnight oil while lawmakers party and play and\nthen\nclaim the credit for the work.\n\nSome of these staffers make pretty good money and get things like free\ninsurance benefits and a coveted parking place inside the Capitol. The golden\nrule of working in the Legislature is to quietly provide the technical\nbackground and let the lawmaker take the credit.\n\nBubbling beneath the surface of every day is the certain knowledge that\nthey\ncan be fired or moved for any reason any time. A client of mine, Labels for Literacy, is a program that is being introduced by the National Federation of the Blind.  Governor John Sununu is greatly involved in this program and I know that you and your family have been great supporters of LITERACY.  \n\n\n\n\n\n\nLabels for Literacy, a project of the American Action Fund for Blind Children and Adults is being supported and promoted by the National Federation of the Blind. The program is aimed at placing Braille and large print labels on standard consumer products and will prompt the sighted population to open their eyes to the facts about the inaccessibility of information available to the blind and visually impaired. This lack of access to information is problematic in its own right, and is also largely responsible for the devastatingly low literacy rate among blind children. As you probably know, since 1963 the percentage of legally blind using Braille as their primary reading medium has dropped from 51 percent to a staggering ten percent.\n\nThis is not due to the lack of learning potential among blind children. The NFB attributes the drop in literacy rates among blind children to the lack of resources and Braille materials available to them. With the advent of audio technology, many children and adults use audio apes in place of books. Although this is a viable medium, Listening is not literacy. There is not enough Braille available to children, said Dr. Maurer, Presidentof the NFB. There is a shortage of Braille in books and in mainstream media. Without available resources, blind children are denied access to information, and the results of this represent a problem that impacts society as a whole. With national initiatives toward equal opportunity, there is also need for this equal opportunity in information access and equal opportunity for Americas blind children to learn to read.\" \n\nOne primary reason that Braille has not been more prevalent thus far is the lack of an efficient technology to produce it. Until now, Braille has been produced by embossing paper or punching indentations onto asheet of paper. The traditional form of Braille is highly susceptible to damage from moisture and pressure, and is a slow and costly process.  A South Florida-based company, TechnologyTree International, has developed a revolutionary new technology that has the ability to produce a more durable Braille at higher speeds and with greater versatility. By utilizing a patented clear epoxy that is printed directly on apage by large commercial presses, the opportunity for the NFB to achieve its goal of having Braille on everything from childrens books to consumers packaged goods in now closer to reality.\n\nBy working closely with Technology Tree International, Daymon Associates Inc., an international food sales and marketing company, national consumer packaged goods companies, and the retail grocery industry, Labels for Literacy will provides self-adhesive sheets of Braille and large print labels to the blind and visually impaired. These labels will allow visually impaired and blind consumers to identify the products they purchase the same way a sighted consumer doesby reading them.  Collectively, the participating consumer packages goods companies and retail grocers are taking the initiative to provide equal access to information for all consumers that purchase their products. Labels for Literacys large print and Braille labels will be distributed free through multiple channels, as well as directly to consumers to use in their homes.    \n                \nWe believe there should be equal opportunity in advertising and labeling for all Americans. Visually impaired, blind and sighted persons should have the same information available to them, said William Kelly, president and CEO of Technology Tree International.  According to Kelly, not only is the technology cost-effective in comparison to the traditionally embossed Braille, there are also benefits in placing the transparent Braille directly on top of printed text. \"This program will aid sighted parents and teachers as they read along in their efforts to teach blind children to read Braille.  To aid in this effort to teach and to understand Braille, we must make more of it available, more Braille in front of kids, more Braille within reach of children sitting at the breakfast table, more Braille on the packages that children pay attention to,  added Kelly.\n\nThis technology opens up the potential for Braille to appear on more mainstream media than ever before  more textbooks, more novels, more public information materials. Peterson \n\n\n    As Americans, we should have been astounded by the recent findings that federal officials intentionally planted hair from the threatened Canadian lynx in our national forests in order to impose sweeping land management regulations. We should have been shocked at the audacity of government employees to falsify evidence in order to advance their environmental agenda, and even more perplexed at the lackluster response from their respective agencies when the transgressions were brought to light. \n\n\n    But in truth, many of us who come from rural America have grown accustomed to environmental activism prevailing over the rule of law and over the best interests of families and communities. Time and time again, we have witnessed the federal government run roughshod over rural America because the Washington elite thought they knew better than the regular folks who tilled the soil, ran the cattle, or enjoyed their favorite campsite or trail. And, if a faulty study or falsified evidence has been necessary in order to enact radical environmental policies, government bureaucrats have had a green light for the past eight years because of the ends-justifies-the-means rationalization that became common practice during the Clinton administration. \n\n\nhttp://www.washingtontimes.com/op-ed/20020107-89178288.htm\n\n\nContent-Transfer-Encoding: Bob Sump, Republican co-chairman of the Natural Resources Committee. \n\n\n    If such a sample had been given, it could have tainted a grizzly habitat study in Washington that encompasses 3,600 square miles and, as a result, affected recreation, timber, mining, road construction and other human activities.   Government scientists planted samples of lynx hairs in a third national forest, according to documents obtained by The Washington Times. \n\n\n    A preliminary investigation by the U.S. Forest Service said planted samples were submitted from the Mount Baker/Snoqualmie National Forest in Washington state, but the report did not say how many additional samples were submitted from that region. Today's (1/3/02) Washington Times. \n\n\nKathleen Benedetto \nProgram Director, NWI \n(703) 836-7404 \n\n\nJanuary 3, 2002 \nScrutiny on lynx fraud grows \n\n\nBy Audrey Hudson \nTHE WASHINGTON TIMES \n\n\n    Federal agencies are teaming up for a joint investigation to determine why government employees planted evidence of a threatened species in national forests. \n                              \n    The fraudulent samples of lynx hair were submitted by five federal and two Washington state employees for laboratory analysis. One of the workers notified his supervisor of the false sampling, and the Forest Service investigated and disciplined the employees. Two other federal employees who worked for the U.S. Fish and Wildlife \nService also were disciplined. \n\n\nhttp://www.washingtontimes.com/national/20020103-13648024.htm \n  \n\n\nLETTERS TO THE EDITOR \nJanuary 3, 2002 \n  \n\n\nEnviornmentalists endanger species  homo sapiens \n\n\n    While I appreciate your justified outrage at the revelation that lynx hairs were planted in national forests in Washington state, we must ask if this is just the tip of the environmental science iceberg (\"The mysterious missing lynx,\" Editorials, Dec. 31). \n                                 \n    This past summer, thousands of farmers and ranchers in southern Oregon and Northern California were denied water predicated upon two biological opinions concerning the short nose sucker and the coho salmon. During a \npublic meeting prior to the water cutoff, I asked the local manager of the U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service three questions: \"How many suckers were in Upper Klamath Lake in 1906?\" (the year the Bureau of Reclamation began operating the Klamath Project); \"How many suckers were in the lake in 1988, at the time they were declared endangered?\"; and \"How many suckers will have to be in the lake in order to be delisted?\" The response to all three questions was, \"We don't know.\" \n                                 \n    It appears that we will continue to have economic train wrecks all over the rural areas of this country until Congress understands that the Endangered Species Act in its present form is a license to steal and destroy the livelihoods of this country's most loyal citizens. In response to Idaho Republican Sen. Larry E. Craig's statement, \"If they [the frauds perpetrators] hadn't been caught, you might have seen entire forests shut down on a false premise,\" and Utah Republican Rep. James V. Hansen's comments that \"this hoax could have wrecked some people's way of life,\" I wish to point out that an agricultural basin has been shut down and that federal action has wrecked many people's lives. And for what? I don't know. \n                                 \nJOHN ELLIOTT \nKlamath County commissioner \nKlamath Falls, Ore. \n                                 \n \n    In your Dec. 31 editorial, \"The mysterious missing lynx,\" you condemn the fraud perpetrated by environmentalist activists working for the government, quoting Idaho Republican Sen. Larry E. Craig: \"If [the frauds perpetrators] hadn't been caught, you might have seen entire forests shut down on a false premise.\" \n                                 \n    However, if the evidence that an \"endangered species\" inhabited the forest had been real, would the entire forest have been shut down on a \"true premise\"? The fraud is worse than planting phony evidence  it's planting phony principles. \n\n\nThe Endangered Species Act (ESA) puts \"nature\" above human rights as a matter of law and, in accord with the environmentalism's inherent nature worship, as a matter of ethics. Its history is one of coercion and corruption of science in the service of biocentrism. \n                                 \n    ESA expired years ago and has been too controversial to be renewed by Congress. Yet Congress, morally and politically intimidated by the environmentalist lobby, continues to fund its implementation. \n\n\n    It's time to kill ESA. The real \"missing lynx\" is the missing connection between ESA and any justification for its existence. \n\n\nERICH VEYHL \nConcord, Mass. \n\n\nhttp://www.washingtontimes.com/op-ed/20020103-12607496.htm#2 \n <http://www.washingtontimes.com/op-ed/20020103-12607496.htm#2>   \n\n\nNOTE: In accordance with Title 17 U.S.C. section 107, any copyrighted material  herein is distributed without profit or payment to those who have expressed  a  prior interest in receiving this information for non-profit research and  educational purposes only. Interior seeks lynx hair probe \nStatus: RO\nMIME-Version: 1.0\n\tboundary=\"--boundary-LibPST-iamunique-2013736943_-_-\"\n\n\n\nDear Folks: \n\n    Included in this e-mail are the first couple of paragraphs from a follow-up story on the Lynx Hoax from the Washington Times and two Letters to the Editor regarding the Lynx hoax that were published in today's paper. \n\n\nKathleen Benedetto \nProgram Director, NWI \n(703) 836-7404 \n\n\nDecember 20, 2001 \n\n\nInterior seeks lynx hair probe \n\n\nBy Audrey Hudson \nTHE WASHINGTON TIMES \n  \n\n\n    Interior Secretary Gale A. Norton yesterday called for an inspector general to investigate why federal biologists planted false evidence of a rare wildcat in two national forests. \n  \n    \"I am deeply troubled by allegations that Fish and Wildlife Service employees, along with employees from other federal and state agencies, deliberately submitted false samples to the laboratory analyzing data for the Canada lynx survey program,\" Mrs. Norton said. \n\n\nhttp://washingtontimes.com/national/20011220-859622.htm \n\n\nLETTERS TO THE EDITOR \nDecember 20, 2001 \n  \n\n\n                           When ends justify means \n\n\n                                Your Dec. 17 story by Audrey Hudson, \"Rare lynx hairs found in forests \n                           exposed as hoax,\" performed a genuine service to all Americans. \n                                For years, radical environmentalists, some in government employment, have \n                           skewed and falsified data in scientific studies to achieve political goals, and no \n                           one was the wiser. Your story certainly lifted the rock and let the sun shine on \n                           that subject. \n                                However, you could have served the public even better by digging further \n                           into the motives of those planting false environmental evidence. \n                                Listening to those on the receiving end of environmental regulations, you \n                           likely would hear that the motives include the shutdown of virtually every \n                           human enterprise and the removal of all human presence from rural areas all \n                           over the nation, not just in the West. \n                                You probably would discover that any hardworking local making such \n                           outlandish accusations inevitably faces an overwhelming outcry of \"conspiracy \n                           theory\" or \"paranoia.\" \n                                You could clarify the subject greatly by reporting on the individuals who \n                           perpetrated this lynx hoax. What did they have in mind? \n                                It might reveal that some conspiracy theories actually reveal conspiracies \n                           and that even if you are paranoid, someone may actually be out to get you. \n                                 \n                                RON ARNOLD \n                                Executive vice president \n                                Center for the Defense of Free Enterprise \n                                Bellevue, Wash. \n  \n  \n\n\n                           Hold federal agencies to a higher standard \n\n\n                                It comes as no surprise to read that federal and state wildlife biologists have \n                           been discovered planting false evidence of the Canadian lynx on National \n                           Forest System lands in Washington state (\"Rare lynx hairs found in forests \n                           exposed as hoax,\" Dec. 17). Locals long familiar with the area told the \n                           \"experts\" that lynx did not inhabit the area, but such evidence apparently is \n                           ignored by a new breed of biologists who know better. \n                                Those of us in the land-use arena have known that oftentimes green groups \n                           will say or do anything to enact their agenda. That is their right. However, \n                           when federal agencies adopt that same credo, it casts a long shadow of doubt \n                           on their land-use-planning efforts, whether those efforts are underpinned by \n                           sound science or are policy-driven. \n                                Events such as this lynx fiasco make it easy to become cynical about the \n                           whole process, and that is sad, given the importance of the agencies' mission. \n                           The blurring of no-holds-barred advocacy and management has politicized the \n                           process and is making it more difficult for agencies to take necessary actions \n                           to manage human activity and protect the environment. \n                                For the past eight years, federal agencies have asked the American people to \n                           take precious time out of their busy schedules to attend a plethora of public \n                           meetings on large land-use-planning efforts. These efforts have as their \n                           foundation the protection of threatened and endangered species. \n                                Millions of taxpayer dollars have been redirected from important recreation \n                           and resource programs at the Department of Interior or the U.S. Forest Service \n                           to fund these endless planning efforts. Some of us said these programs had no \n                           built-in safeguards to protect our access to federal lands by unscrupulous or \n                           agenda-driven agency biologists. We were told not to worry. \n                                Elected officials and agency leadership must demonstrate a zero-tolerance \n                           policy for behavior like that in this lynx issue. On too many occasions, agency \n                           personnel caught misusing appropriated tax monies to enact the anti-access \n                           agenda of the environmental movement have avoided public accountability for \n                           their actions. The American public and the mission deserve more. \n                                I once heard a popular green proverb that said, \"Our agenda is so important \n                           that we have to lie.\" Though that philosophy may be understandable for some \n                           groups, I hope our nation and elected officials will hold the federal land \n                           agencies to a higher standard. \n                                 \n                                DON AMADOR \n                                Oakley, Calif. \n\n\nhttp://www.washingtontimes.com/op-ed/20011220-3705892.htm#4 \n\n\nNOTE: In accordance with Title 17 U.S.C. section 107, any copyrighted material  herein is distributed without profit or payment to those who have expressed  a  prior interest in receiving this information for non-profit research and  educational purposes only. The report, portions of which were leaked to reporters and advocates in recent weeks, comes at a critical time, though its recommendations are nonbinding. \n\n\nThe panel, called the Occupational Access and Opportunity Commission, has indicated in recent weeks it plans to move forward with privatization, regardless of recent audits and reports suggesting the shift hasn't benefited disabled Floridians or taxpayers. Only the Legislature can dismantle the commission. \n\n\nIn fact, on Friday the commission will discuss plans to move ahead with the shift to private management in much of the state. So far, services have been privatized in 10 counties -- four rural North Florida counties, Bradford and Alachua, and the Treasure Coast counties of Indian River, Martin, Okeechobee and St. Lucie. \n\n\nGov. Jeb Bush said Tuesday he is waiting to learn more about the audit's findings before deciding whether he will take any action. He said he has requested a briefing next week from education department leaders.\n\n\n\nAWAITING A RESPONSE \n\n\nFlorida Education Commissioner Charlie Crist, whose agency oversees Florida's $125 million vocational rehabilitation effort, said he is awaiting a response to the report from Carl Miller, who manages the program. \n\n\n``It's disturbing to me,'' Crist said of the audit. ``I don't want to pass any judgment on this thing. . . . But what I see in the report is disturbing. . . . Clients are not being served well -- and maybe not taxpayers.'' \n\n\nOAOC Chairman Dennis Celorie of Sarasota said he will lobby at Friday's meeting for giving the 16-member commission another year to solve its problems and launch a more well-reasoned shift toward private management. ``I think the commission can handle what's going on,'' he said. \n\n\nAuditors said the privatization effort, though well-intended, has fared badly because the commission charged with its implementation planned and managed the transition poorly. \n\n\nIndeed, the agency that controls Florida's $100 million in federal vocational rehab dollars has labeled Florida the only state at ``high risk'' of losing its federal funding -- two years in a row. \n\n\n``If it had been properly planned as intended by the Legislature and more carefully managed, the effort could have served as a model for other states and may have been more favorably received by the federal government,'' auditors wrote. ``Although required to do so by state law, the commission did not develop a five-year plan for the program.'' \n\n\nPrivate providers were given large start-up costs to develop their programs, though the OAOC never required the businesses to document or justify those awards, the auditors reported. \n\n\nMoreover, the commission never developed a formula for measuring whether the private providers were doing better work, for less money, than their counterparts in state government, the report states. \n\n\n``The net effect of these contracting problems is that service costs substantially increased in the three demonstration regions, while accountability for program funds substantially decreased,'' the report concluded. \n\n\nFor clients of the agency, the transition has led to problems, as well. Fewer disabled Floridians have been screened for eligibility for services in the last two years, the report says, and such determinations are ``projected to fall significantly in the 2001-2002 fiscal year.'' The number of clients matched with jobs fell as well. \n\n\nAs the OAOC pushed ``aggressively'' toward private management, they gave public counselors the distinct message their services were no longer wanted, the report says, though the original intent of lawmakers was to offer disabled Floridians a choice between public and private counselors.\n\n\n\nMASSIVE TURNOVER \n\n\nAs a result, turnover among public counselors reached unprecedented levels, forcing clients to seek new case managers often. In the fiscal year 1999-2000, for example, 28 percent of the program's counselors left. The next year, according to the report, 19 percent quit. \n\n\nIrwin M. Kaplan, a Pompano Beach man with physical and emotional disabilities, went through three counselors in less than two years, he told The Herald. \n\n\n``A worker who might have had 60 cases ends up with 150 cases,'' Kaplan lamented. ``Clients get lost in the shuffle. It slows things down.'' \n\n\nMarvin Rosenblatt, a Broward psychologist who has worked with Kaplan, said the disruptions proved particularly taxing for Kaplan, who suffers from extreme anxiety that was exacerbated by the uncertainly over his care. \n\n\n``He had a medical problem requiring surgery,'' Rosenblatt said. ``His counselor had assured him he would get this help. But a new counselor came in and had to go through a series of steps to determine whether or not this would be done. He was afraid he might lose his job.'' \n\n\nThe report was a sort of vindication for OAOC Commissioner Warren Jernigan, a disabled Pensacola man who for years had urged the board to move cautiously. Jernigan voted against proceeding with new private contracts. \n\n\n``We've got to slow down so that we make sure we capture the data necessary to justify our decisions,'' Jernigan said Tuesday after reviewing the draft report. ``My concern is for the disabled, and for the future of this program.'' \n\n\nHerald staff writer Joni James contributed to this report.","id":"20020112-20020112.125038.-1712309.3.AponteMB@juno.com","subject":"VR - OAOC","dateCentral":"2002-01-12T11:50:34-06:00"},{"from":"Sid Dinerstein <siddinerstein@yahoo.com>","to":"jeb bush <jeb@jeb.org>","date":"Sat, 12 Jan 2002 10:00:52 -0800 (PST)","message":"Dear Governor Bush:\n\nAs the Vice Chairman of the Republican Party of Palm\nBeach County I want to thank you for the School Board\nappointment of Ed Garcia. The report, portions of which were leaked to reporters and advocates in recent weeks, comes at a critical time, though its recommendations are nonbinding. \n\n\nThe panel, called the Occupational Access and Opportunity Commission, has indicated in recent weeks it plans to move forward with privatization, regardless of recent audits and reports suggesting the shift hasn't benefited disabled Floridians or taxpayers. Only the Legislature can dismantle the commission. \n\n\nIn fact, on Friday the commission will discuss plans to move ahead with the shift to private management in much of the state. So far, services have been privatized in 10 counties -- four rural North Florida counties, Bradford and Alachua, and the Treasure Coast counties of Indian River, Martin, Okeechobee and St. Lucie. \n\n\nGov. Jeb Bush said Tuesday he is waiting to learn more about the audit's findings before deciding whether he will take any action. He said he has requested a briefing next week from education department leaders.\n\n\n\nAWAITING A RESPONSE \n\n\nFlorida Education Commissioner Charlie Crist, whose agency oversees Florida's $125 million vocational rehabilitation effort, said he is awaiting a response to the report from Carl Miller, who manages the program. \n\n\n``It's disturbing to me,'' Crist said of the audit. ``I don't want to pass any judgment on this thing. . . . But what I see in the report is disturbing. . . . Clients are not being served well -- and maybe not taxpayers.'' \n\n\nOAOC Chairman Dennis Celorie of Sarasota said he will lobby at Friday's meeting for giving the 16-member commission another year to solve its problems and launch a more well-reasoned shift toward private management. ``I think the commission can handle what's going on,'' he said. \n\n\nAuditors said the privatization effort, though well-intended, has fared badly because the commission charged with its implementation planned and managed the transition poorly. \n\n\nIndeed, the agency that controls Florida's $100 million in federal vocational rehab dollars has labeled Florida the only state at ``high risk'' of losing its federal funding -- two years in a row. \n\n\n``If it had been properly planned as intended by the Legislature and more carefully managed, the effort could have served as a model for other states and may have been more favorably received by the federal government,'' auditors wrote. ``Although required to do so by state law, the commission did not develop a five-year plan for the program.'' \n\n\nPrivate providers were given large start-up costs to develop their programs, though the OAOC never required the businesses to document or justify those awards, the auditors reported. \n\n\nMoreover, the commission never developed a formula for measuring whether the private providers were doing better work, for less money, than their counterparts in state government, the report states. \n\n\n``The net effect of these contracting problems is that service costs substantially increased in the three demonstration regions, while accountability for program funds substantially decreased,'' the report concluded. \n\n\nFor clients of the agency, the transition has led to problems, as well. Fewer disabled Floridians have been screened for eligibility for services in the last two years, the report says, and such determinations are ``projected to fall significantly in the 2001-2002 fiscal year.'' The number of clients matched with jobs fell as well. \n\n\nAs the OAOC pushed ``aggressively'' toward private management, they gave public counselors the distinct message their services were no longer wanted, the report says, though the original intent of lawmakers was to offer disabled Floridians a choice between public and private counselors.\n\n\n\nMASSIVE TURNOVER \n\n\nAs a result, turnover among public counselors reached unprecedented levels, forcing clients to seek new case managers often. In the fiscal year 1999-2000, for example, 28 percent of the program's counselors left. The next year, according to the report, 19 percent quit. \n\n\nIrwin M. Kaplan, a Pompano Beach man with physical and emotional disabilities, went through three counselors in less than two years, he told The Herald. \n\n\n``A worker who might have had 60 cases ends up with 150 cases,'' Kaplan lamented. ``Clients get lost in the shuffle. It slows things down.'' \n\n\nMarvin Rosenblatt, a Broward psychologist who has worked with Kaplan, said the disruptions proved particularly taxing for Kaplan, who suffers from extreme anxiety that was exacerbated by the uncertainly over his care. \n\n\n``He had a medical problem requiring surgery,'' Rosenblatt said. ``His counselor had assured him he would get this help. But a new counselor came in and had to go through a series of steps to determine whether or not this would be done. He was afraid he might lose his job.'' \n\n\nThe report was a sort of vindication for OAOC Commissioner Warren Jernigan, a disabled Pensacola man who for years had urged the board to move cautiously. Jernigan voted against proceeding with new private contracts. \n\n\n``We've got to slow down so that we make sure we capture the data necessary to justify our decisions,'' Jernigan said Tuesday after reviewing the draft report. ``My concern is for the disabled, and for the future of this program.'' \n\n\nHerald staff writer Joni James contributed to this report.","id":"20020112-E43F6E09953F9D4BA2A77F4B243A4ED0010E236B@exch2000.jeb.org","subject":"RE: VR - OAOC fop","dateCentral":"2002-01-12T13:36:22-06:00"},{"from":"\"Jeb Bush\" <MAILER-DAEMON>","to":"'Sid Dinerstein'","date":"Sat, 12 Jan 2002 20:34:08 +0000","message":"thank you Sid.\n\nJeb Bush \n\n***********************************************************\nPlease note: Florida has a very broad public records law.\nMost written communications to or from state officials\nregarding state business are public records available to the\npublic and media upon request. St.\nPete Times 1/12/02\n\n\n\n\n-----Original Message-----\nFrom: Valerie Adams [mailto:valadams@tampabay.rr.com]\nSent: Saturday, January 12, 2002 9:31 AM\nTo: Martha Pratt\nSubject: Opinion: Florida sliding back to its Old South ways. St. Pete\nTimes 1/12/02\n\n\nOpinion.\n\nFlorida sliding back to its Old South ways\nBy DIANE ROBERTS\n St. Petersburg Times\npublished January 12, 2002\n--------------------------------------------------------------------\n\nTALLAHASSEE -- William Faulkner once said, \"In the South, the past is never\ndead, it's not even past.\" Faulkner, Mississippi's second-best product\n(after the Delta Blues), knew what he was talking about: His great-aunts\nwould get up and walk out of Gone With the Wind the minute Gen. Sherman's\nname was mentioned.\n\nFlorida likes to think of itself as New South -- if it admits to being\nSouthern at all. But old times here are not forgotten, either. Journalists\nacross the state recently received an e-mail from the League of the South\nexhorting them to mark Jan. 10 as \"Florida Freedom Day.\" On that date in\n1861, a not-very-democratic cadre of planters and other rich white folks got\ntogether in Tallahassee and voted to secede from the Union.\n\nWe all remember how well that worked out.\n\nIn his message, Steve Walker, \"Provisional Chairman\" of the Northeast\nFlorida League of the South, says it was \"an oppressive national government\"\nthat drove the state to \"declare her independence\" 141 years ago, and urges\nthe press to \"proclaim her day\" in headlines.\n\nIn case you've missed some of their other crusades (rallying for the\nConfederate battle flag, calling the renaming of a street for Rev. Martin\nLuther King a \"heritage violation,\" insisting the Civil War had nothing to\ndo with slavery) the League of the South is an organization advocating the\nre-secession of Southern states to form a \"Christian Republic\" where men are\nmen, women are ladies and black people are welcome as long as they don't get\nuppity. The league loathes multiculturalism, feminism, secularism and taxes\nof all kinds. You might note a number of similarities to far-right fringe of\nthe Florida Republican Party.\n\nProgressive Democratic governors such as LeRoy Collins, Reuben Askew and Bob\nGraham did much to lead the state out of the torpid and hateful days of Jim\nCrow, fighting for integration, fair courts, equal rights, access to quality\neducation and health care. But now for all its superficial New South patina,\nthe state is sliding back to its Old South ways.\n\nAt the secession convention of 1861, the rulers of Florida were obsessed\nwith states' rights: chiefly the \"right\" of one group of people to own\nanother group of people. Now what the rulers of Florida care most about is\nthe \"right\" of the affluent to pay as few taxes as possible, no matter who\nsuffers. It wasn't just the recession or the attacks of Sept. 11 that hurt\nthe state's economy, it's two years of tax cuts, sponsored by Gov. Jeb Bush\nand the Republican-controlled Legislature. In this failure of\nresponsibility, you could say that Florida has seceded from a commitment to\na just society.\n\nWe have seceded from decent education funding. In the recent budget-slashing\nfrenzy of two special sessions, our Republican-led government has zeroed-out\nmoney to recruit and retain new teachers. Florida used to want to compare\nitself to New York, Michigan, Massachusetts and California in the quality of\nits public education. Now we do worse than Georgia and North Carolina. And\nwe are losing teachers to better-paying jobs in Alabama.\n\nWe have seceded from a serious university system. By junking the Board of\nRegents in favor of politicized boards of trustees, we will have warring\ntribes competing for each pitiful higher education dollar. Tuition will go\nup, making it even harder for lower-income students to afford a four-year\ndegree.\n\nWe have seceded from responsibility for the poor and the old. The\nLegislature cut prenatal care to women who will never own stocks and bonds\nand so aren't too bothered by the intangibles tax -- now they just have to\nworry about bringing a healthy baby into the world. And 23,000 elderly\npeople will have to find a way to pay for their own prescription drugs\nbecause of the budget cuts. Many of them will just do without. This coming\nlegislative session, there's no telling what Florida will secede from. There\nare bills filed that will hamstring Florida's strong public records laws,\nallowing the government to close arrest records and keep many of its\noperations secret. There are legislators hell-bent on once again attacking\nFlorida's independent judiciary. They want judges appointed solely by the\ngovernor or elected in partisan races -- just like they were 30 or 40 years\nago when Florida's legal system was a disgraceful swamp of corruption and\ngraft.\n\nMaybe the biggest difference between the League of the South and the\nradicals running Florida is that the league at least understands and\nacknowledges that they want to return to a pre-modern society. Jeb Bush and\nTom Feeney (John McKay, the senate president, actually has ideas for tax\nreform, making him a bit of a freak in the party) seem convinced that they\nare taking Florida forward. But forward to what? Some kind of banana\nrepublic where the rich get richer, the poor get poorer, and everybody who\ncan goes to college in Georgia?\n\nMaybe we should commemorate (though not celebrate) the day in 1861 the\nrulers of Florida chose to separate the state from the core American values\nof liberty and justice for all. Remembering our history might at least slow\ndown our repeating it.\n\n- Diane Roberts, a former Times editorial writer, is a professor of English\nat the University of Alabama.","id":"20020112-311EC6B4B5614744B91D69784B12250B287A61@exch2000.jeb.org","subject":"FW: Opinion: Florida sliding back to its Old South ways. The language of this Bill has in it a method for districts to raise money through a surtax. But SB382 refers to Capital Expenses ONLY! \nCapital money is used primarily to pay for building construction and renovation, but not building maintenance. Capital money also covers the cost of other big-ticket items such as school buses and computers (SAP/R3).\nUnlike county and municipal governments, school districts cannot mix capital and operating expenses. Operating funds cover the day-to-day expense of running the school district. Salaries, supplies, student transportation, and building maintenance are all included in the operating budget. \n  \nThis means NONE of this money derived from the surtax could be used for anything other than capital expenses...new schools and school renovations! NO WAGE INCREASES FROM THIS TAX>>>PERIOD!\nThe amount of information about this surtax in this Bill is NOT reaching the citizens of this state, and so there is this mistaken belief that by supporting a surtax increase for the school district it would help the teacher wage problem, or help in reducing the student/teacher ratio, or help in keeping those truant officers employed in order to reduce the dropout rates, or any thing else the citizens may have thought. \nWRONG!\n \nThis Bill WILL help build more schools, and make the building of those new schools better because corners will not have to be cut, because of funding. But what this will ultimately do is create unusable square footage of building space...BECAUSE...the state continues to short school districts on operating funding! AND the school district's school board continues to go unchecked with their un-satisfiable appetite for spending operating funds in a crazy manner.\n \nThe rest of the SB382 Bill looks pretty good as it puts increased pressure on the growth management issue going unchecked, and it FORCES the builders, county governments, and school districts to work together in reducing the problems incurred with unchecked growth.
